But she said nothing. She played with the ribbon of her typewriter.

‚ÄúI hate staying in one place,‚Äù said Caroline, after a moment of silence.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs no fun traveling,‚Äù said Robert Holton. ‚ÄúMoving around all the time; that‚Äôs what I didn‚Äôt like in the army. No, traveling‚Äôs pretty lousy.‚Äù

[Pg 42]

‚ÄúThat kind is, but I mean to go ... well, you know ... where you want to go, that‚Äôs what I mean. I don‚Äôt like sitting around here day after day. I want to go some place.‚Äù

He shrugged. ‚ÄúA lot of people do, I guess. Marjorie, you know, the waitress, she wants to go to Sicily.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, that‚Äôs different. I mean she‚Äôs not ... well, you know what I mean, she‚Äôs probably happy doing what she‚Äôs doing.‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt see why,‚Äù said Robert Holton. They thought of Marjorie Ventusa for a moment then they didn‚Äôt think of her again.

Robert Holton shifted his position on the railing. Caroline looked about the familiar room. The older women were typing and using their adding machines; the younger women were watching Robert Holton; and the younger men (there were three of them) looked up occasionally to see what Caroline was doing. She posed a little for them. She didn‚Äôt pose haughtily, though. Caroline was too clever for that. She just looked girlish and rather innocent. None of them could understand her sadness and her longing. It pleased her to think how well she hid herself. Not even Robert Holton, talking to her now, could realize these things.

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù said Robert Holton, ‚Äúno, I want to stay in one place.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou don‚Äôt want to be doing the same thing all the time, do you?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know, I‚Äôd like to make more money.‚Äù

‚ÄúI think you‚Äôre crazy,‚Äù she said. She watched her fingers as they tapped lightly on the keys of the typewriter. Her hands weren‚Äôt quite what she wanted them to be. She thought of them as long and slender and faintly exotic; actually her hands were short and square and not very clean. The red enamel was beginning to chip off her thumbnails.

[Pg 43]

‚ÄúWhy‚Äôm I crazy? Because I want to make more money?‚Äù

‚ÄúNot because of that, of course. Just because.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh.‚Äù

Robert Holton shifted his position on the railing. Caroline suddenly didn‚Äôt want him to go. Then Richard Kuppelton got up from his desk near the door and came over to them.

‚ÄúWhy, hello, Dick,‚Äù said Caroline.

‚ÄúGood morning, good morning,‚Äù said Dick heartily. He was a very hearty person and Caroline liked him. He was so different from Robert Holton. Dick always seemed the same; he acted the same, anyway. Caroline could almost always tell what he was going to say and that was a lot better than being around a person who never said the right things. Dick wasn‚Äôt sensitive, however. He and Robert Holton were the same that way but then Caroline couldn‚Äôt have everything.

‚ÄúHow‚Äôs every little thing?‚Äù asked Dick Kuppelton.

‚ÄúFine,‚Äù said Robert Holton. Caroline only smiled; she smiled with her eyes as well as her mouth. It was important to smile that way.

‚ÄúBeen pretty slow today,‚Äù said Dick. ‚ÄúNot much business. I think the market‚Äôs falling off.‚Äù Someone had told him that, thought Caroline, delighted with her perception.

‚ÄúIt may be,‚Äù said Robert Holton without much interest.

‚ÄúWe should have a big rush soon. I‚Äôm doing a report now. Well, not really a report; I‚Äôve been getting some statistics on aircraft stock ready for the front office. It‚Äôs been some job.‚Äù He shook his head to show the largeness of the job.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve got a report like that to do, too,‚Äù said Caroline.

‚ÄúSomething for Golden?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

Dick nodded knowingly. ‚ÄúSome report, I bet.‚Äù

[Pg 44]

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs certainly long,‚Äù said Caroline, pointing to the notebook on her desk.

Robert Holton got off the railing and stretched. ‚ÄúI better get to work,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúMurphy might be back soon.‚Äù He went back to his desk.

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs real eager,‚Äù said Dick unpleasantly.

‚ÄúWhat? Well, I don‚Äôt know about that. He‚Äôs sort of funny. He doesn‚Äôt want to get anywhere but he doesn‚Äôt want to get in bad. I don‚Äôt know; he‚Äôs awful funny.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôve seen those guys before,‚Äù said Dick. ‚ÄúI know that type. They come in a place and get in good with the top people. Then they get your job. That‚Äôs just what he‚Äôs up to.‚Äù

Caroline smiled and said nothing. She was pretty and popular and she couldn‚Äôt always, therefore, say what she thought. She knew, though, that Dick Kuppelton, who had been with Heywood and Golden for six years, disliked Holton. Mr Murphy had never liked Kuppelton and at the end of the year changes were always made and Robert Holton might take Dick‚Äôs place. Things were very complicated, thought Caroline.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt think he‚Äôs that smart,‚Äù said Caroline.

‚ÄúI think you‚Äôre wrong.‚Äù Dick started to straddle the railing, then he changed his mind and leaned against it. He was a large man. He was thirty and pink and blond. He wore large rimless glasses which made his face look clean and blank. He enjoyed what he was doing, thought Caroline. Everyone enjoyed working except herself.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve got to do some typing,‚Äù said Caroline. She wanted him to go away.

‚ÄúCertainly; I suppose I‚Äôd better be getting back.‚Äù He stood up straight and stretched. ‚ÄúWell, back to work,‚Äù he said.

[Pg 45]

‚ÄúSee you,‚Äù said Caroline. Dick was so dependable: you always knew what to expect.

Caroline coughed. Her cough had a consumptive sound to it which rather appealed to her. When she was a young girl she had seen a play about a beautiful woman with white flowers and a cough. The beautiful woman had been so interesting that Caroline had never forgotten her although she had forgotten the play. Caroline coughed again, quietly, dramatically.

‚ÄúHow‚Äôs that report coming?‚Äù Oliver L. Murphy had returned from the front office.

‚ÄúPretty well, Mr Murphy.‚Äù

‚ÄúHad quite a session with Mr Golden.‚Äù

‚ÄúI bet,‚Äù said Caroline with sympathy. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll bet he was something.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, I handled him O.K. today. He‚Äôs not so hard to get along with. Of course, he‚Äôs got some queer ideas. Those people often have.‚Äù

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt that the truth.‚Äù Caroline arranged the paper in her typewriter. Mr Murphy leaned over and smelled the carnation in the imitation silver vase.

‚ÄúSmells nice, don‚Äôt it?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt certainly does, Mr Murphy.‚Äù She smiled. Mr Murphy went back to his desk and Caroline typed. Several times as she worked she coughed, quietly, almost to herself.

[Pg 46]

Chapter Four

Richard Kuppelton left Caroline reluctantly. He liked her because she was pretty and much more sensible than the other pretty girls he had known.

He stopped at his desk. It was a dull olive color. His different books of statistics were piled neatly on one corner; notebooks and papers were scattered over the top and it looked as if he were busy.

Kuppelton decided not to work, not just now. From the top drawer of his desk he took a magazine. It had a vivid cover of a large-breasted young woman being carried into a machine by an octopus. He enjoyed this magazine‚Äôs stories very much.

He slipped the magazine under his arm, the cover toward his side; and then, busily, he left the room for the lavatory.

There was something cozy about a lavatory, he thought as he opened the door marked ‚ÄúMen.‚Äù No one was inside and he would be able to sing. The room was large, white and very clean. The urinals, four of them, stood polished and shining, like soldiers on guard. A thin waterfall constantly descended down their white enamel surfaces; the smell of disinfectant was in the air, but not too strongly.

[Pg 47]

Richard Kuppelton glanced at himself quickly in one of the four mirrors which shone over the four wash basins. Then he walked to one of the four black-doored stalls. He chose the one nearest the wall. There was strategy in his choice as well as habit, for the light was over this stall.

With the feeling of having come home after a long journey, Richard Kuppelton opened the black door and stepped inside. Then he closed the door and locked it. He was completely alone now; no one could disturb him and he was safe.

Deliberately he hung up his coat and then, after some preparation, he descended with a sigh upon the cool smooth seat. He relaxed happily.

On the subway he had started a story called ‚ÄúThe Mad Moon Maidens‚Äù; unfortunately, it had been a little dull and he had decided not to finish it. He thumbed through the rough pages of his magazine. Grotesque black and white drawings decorated the pages. There were monsters and ghouls, beautiful women (usually screaming) and lean young men with pongee hats. The title ‚ÄúSatanic Underworld‚Äù appealed to him and he started to read.

After only a few minutes, however, he found himself studying the tile floor. Black and white tile in neat one-two-three pattern across the floor; he liked things that were black or white. The pattern was familiar to him and gave him a further feeling of being home.

Great ideas came to Richard Kuppelton enthroned. Here in this retreat the entire world assumed a pattern of great simplicity. All problems could be rendered answerable and in this world he was sovereign. The lavatory was his study. He thought of Robert Holton: the person who currently threatened his career.

[Pg 48]

Robert Holton was deceitful; he knew that. On the surface he appeared simple and a little shy but Kuppelton knew differently. Little things that the others had not noticed he noticed. For instance, Holton was always trying to get friendly with Mr Murphy. He always called him ‚Äúsir‚Äù; treated him as if he were a colonel or something in the army. That was another thing: the army. Holton had been a soldier and Kuppelton had not. Most of the others in the office had not been in the war either. Both Mr Heywood and Mr Golden had declared that they would do all that they could for the veteran. So far this hadn‚Äôt been very much, but still it was their intention. Richard Kuppelton wished suddenly that he could stay forever in this shiny black stall with the tile floor.

There was a noise in the lavatory. Someone had come in. Footsteps clattered on the floor. The door to the stall next to his opened and someone sat down.

He wondered who it was. The person wore plain brown shoes: he could see them through the foot-high space beneath the stall partition. This person also wore brown trousers. Richard Kuppelton thought for a moment, strained to remember who it could be. Then he remembered.

‚ÄúHello, Bob,‚Äù said Richard Kuppelton.

‚ÄúWhat? That you, Dick?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe same.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou catching up on your reading?‚Äù

Richard Kuppelton closed his magazine guiltily. ‚ÄúNo, no. Just nature.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a good place to think.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, I suppose it is.‚Äù

[Pg 49]

‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs wrong with Caroline today?‚Äù asked Robert Holton.

‚ÄúI haven‚Äôt the slightest idea. I didn‚Äôt notice anything wrong with her, did you?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, I thought she was sort of irritable.‚Äù

‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt notice it.‚Äù Richard Kuppelton sighed. He was beginning to get uncomfortable, sitting on the hard seat. He was, also, a little surprised that Holton was as aware of Caroline as this. ‚ÄúCaroline‚Äôs a lot of fun,‚Äù he said.

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe‚Äôs a lot of fun to go out on a party with. She can be real funny.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose so.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou ever go out with her?‚Äù

‚ÄúNot really.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat do you mean?‚Äù

‚ÄúI never went to a party with her. We had dinner once.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe didn‚Äôt want to go dancing?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs funny.‚Äù Richard Kuppelton tried to remember whether he had ever taken Caroline out and they had not danced. No, they had always gone to a dance. He wondered whether she liked Robert Holton better than him. This was a new thought and even more unpleasant than the suspicion that Robert Holton was trying to get his job. ‚ÄúShe just likes to talk?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, I guess everybody does.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right, I guess.‚Äù Richard Kuppelton studied Holton‚Äôs plain tan shoes gloomily. One of the things he could not understand was why Robert Holton had come to work in this office. It was rumored that he was a friend of Mr[Pg 50] Heywood‚Äôs but no one had ever been able to prove that. He had gone to Harvard before the war and to Richard Kuppelton that was the most important thing about him. It was also suspicious; he could not understand why a person with that education would do this job in Heywood and Golden unless—and Richard Kuppelton became gloomier—unless he were to be promoted over everyone.

‚ÄúLooks like there‚Äôll be a lot of changes after the first,‚Äù said Kuppelton.

‚ÄúThey tell me there usually are.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose you want to end up in the other office, being one of the contact people.‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt care much. Whatever they want to do. I‚Äôd like to move up, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe all would.‚Äù

Robert Holton mumbled something and stood up. Kuppelton watched the tan shoes as they moved about the stall. There was a swirling of water and Robert Holton left the lavatory, whistling.

Richard Kuppelton studied the tile again. It seemed, somehow, less comforting, less private since Holton had been here. He tried to read again but ‚ÄúSatanic Underworld‚Äù had lost its attraction. The seat was becoming harder every minute and he would have to leave soon.

Then he remembered that the acoustics were unusually good in this lavatory. In a low voice he sang an Irish ballad which he had learned in school. His voice came to him pure and vibrant and like no other voice that had ever sung. He finished with a low note, although, strictly speaking, the ballad called for a high note. He sang a popular song next. It was not as great a success as the first because he only knew the[Pg 51] chorus. The words that he made up, however, were quite good enough.

At last, his songs finished, Richard Kuppelton stood up. He ached slightly from the strain of sitting on the narrow seat. Deliberately he arranged his trousers, deploring slightly the heaviness of his waist as he did.

The sound of swirling water was in his ears as he crossed the lavatory to the wash basin. Deliberately—he was a deliberate person—he washed his hands. He dried his hands on a paper towel and then, like a king abdicating, he moved slowly but deliberately to the door. With a sigh Richard Kuppelton left the lavatory.

The office had not changed. Mr Murphy was sitting behind his railing, smoking a cigar and reading a letter. Caroline was typing. Robert Holton was copying a row of figures into his notebook. The other men and women in the office were working busily.

Richard Kuppelton sat down at his desk. He enjoyed the sensation of being a part of this great house. Neatly he arranged his books of tables and statistics across the top of his desk. The various books were open at aircraft stock. His statistics would form the basis of a report which would be used in an overall survey of aircraft stock to be used by the front office. His responsibilities were heavy.

He took his fountain pen out of his pocket. It was leaking a little and he had to handle it carefully. Slowly, with pleasure, he copied the figures from the books. He wrote the numbers carefully, making them round and legible. When he had finished copying all his numbers they would be typed up by one of the stenographers in the office.

A tall white-faced boy in a blue suit came into the room.[Pg 52] He went to Richard Kuppelton's desk and put some papers on it.

‚ÄúGood morning, Jim,‚Äù said Kuppelton heartily. ‚ÄúHow‚Äôs the boy?‚Äù

‚ÄúFine. I think Golden‚Äôs coming this way.‚Äù

‚ÄúReally? Wonder what he wants.‚Äù

‚ÄúHard to say. He always wants something.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs his privilege,‚Äù said Kuppelton righteously.

‚ÄúI suppose so,‚Äù said Jim.

The white-faced boy went on to the next desk, handing out letters and inter-office memoranda.

Richard Kuppelton put his fountain pen down carefully. There were several letters for him. He opened one of them and started to read.

He had read only a few lines when Mr Golden came into the office. Even without looking up from his letter Richard Kuppelton could have told that someone from the front office had arrived. The typewriters clattered more loudly. The usual low buzz of voices died away, and he could hear Mr Murphy‚Äôs swivel chair being pushed back from his desk as he stood up to welcome the visitor from the front office. Kuppelton put his letter under the blotter and then he looked up casually.

Benjamin Franklin Golden stood behind Mr Murphy‚Äôs railing. He stood very erect, his eyes moving from desk to desk as he studied the office. He was a short man and plump. His eyes were small and black and shiny. Mr Golden had iron-gray hair which he allowed to grow a little longer than necessary. He was proud to have kept his hair. He had a small nose and a rather foolish little mouth and he looked[Pg 53] more like a South American or Italian or something like that, thought Kuppelton.

He pretended to write figures in his notebook, while he listened carefully to what Mr Golden was saying to Mr Murphy.

‚ÄúEverything all right here, Murphy?‚Äù Mr Golden had a high thin voice.

‚ÄúYes, sir, we‚Äôre getting your reports out. I‚Äôll have the special one for you this afternoon.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs good. I really need that report. That‚Äôs an important one. Some of our big steel clients are interested in it. I know you‚Äôve done a good job on it.‚Äù There was almost a threat in his voice. It was well known that the two did not like each other.

‚ÄúWell, I‚Äôve got our best girl, I‚Äôve got Caroline here typing it.‚Äù He waved at Caroline who looked up and smiled at Mr Golden who smiled back at her. Richard Kuppelton wondered what Mrs Golden was like.

‚ÄúI‚Äôm sure she‚Äôll do a good job. How‚Äôs that aircraft stock report coming?‚Äù

‚ÄúKuppelton‚Äôs doing it.‚Äù Mr Murphy pointed to him.

Mr Golden nodded. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll be interested to see it.‚Äù Richard Kuppelton copied figures quickly.

‚ÄúShould be a good survey,‚Äù said Mr Murphy. ‚ÄúIs there going to be a board meeting this morning? You said they hadn‚Äôt decided earlier.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, yes, I almost forgot; there‚Äôll be a meeting at eleven-thirty.‚Äù Mr Golden had an irritatingly brusque manner.

‚ÄúFine,‚Äù said Mr Murphy and he made a note of it on the pad on his desk.

[Pg 54]

Mr Golden didn‚Äôt seem to want to go. He looked around the room again. He looked at Robert Holton and said something to Mr Murphy which Kuppelton couldn‚Äôt hear. Mr Murphy smiled and nodded.

Mr Golden finally opened the door of the railing. ‚ÄúSee you at the meeting, Murphy.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir.‚Äù

Mr Golden hurried out of the office. There was an immediate change in the sounds of the room after he had left. The hum of voices began again. Richard Kuppelton put down his fountain pen.

Caroline and Mr Murphy were talking together and laughing. Robert Holton was still working quietly at his desk. The women of the office talked about Mr Golden in low voices.

Richard Kuppelton wondered what Mr Golden had said to Mr Murphy about Robert Holton. He looked at Robert Holton with dislike.

‚ÄúO.K.,‚Äù said Kuppelton, ‚ÄúMr Golden‚Äôs gone, you can stop working.‚Äù

Robert Holton put down his notebook and smiled. ‚ÄúIt doesn‚Äôt hurt,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúIt doesn‚Äôt hurt to look busy.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, no, I wasn‚Äôt meaning to criticize.‚Äù

‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt think you were. Did you hear what they were talking about?‚Äù

This was malicious, Richard Kuppelton knew; it would have been very hard for Holton not to have heard. ‚ÄúOh, they were just talking about reports.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs what I guessed.‚Äù He started to work again.

‚ÄúYou live uptown, don‚Äôt you?‚Äù remarked Kuppelton.

‚ÄúYes. I‚Äôve got a room in a hotel.‚Äù

[Pg 55]

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs funny, I thought you lived with your family or something. I thought Caroline said something about it.‚Äù

‚ÄúMy father used to live here. He lives in Boston now. He used to work here but he retired when I got out of the army.‚Äù

Richard Kuppelton nodded. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right, I remember your telling me that once. Me, I live with all my family in Queens. We all live there. I wish sometimes that I lived alone.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not much fun, living alone,‚Äù said Robert Holton.

‚ÄúThink you‚Äôll get married soon?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt think so.‚Äù

‚ÄúI think I might,‚Äù said Richard Kuppelton weightily; he had no one in mind, though; except possibly Caroline.

‚ÄúI guess it‚Äôs a good idea if you‚Äôve got the right person,‚Äù said Robert Holton.

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs very true.‚Äù They thought of this a moment. Each thought of it seriously and each regarded it distantly. Richard Kuppelton had no real desire to be married. He supposed that Robert Holton felt the same.

‚ÄúI wonder,‚Äù said Kuppelton subtly, ‚Äúwhat the conference is going to be about this afternoon. I wonder if it‚Äôs about promotions in the departments.‚Äù

‚ÄúI haven‚Äôt any idea.‚Äù

‚ÄúSince the war, seniority doesn‚Äôt make much difference.‚Äù

‚ÄúI thought it did.‚Äù

Kuppelton shook his head, convinced of Holton‚Äôs insincerity. For weeks now everyone had discussed the new policy and everyone had watched the veterans in the different offices, especially Holton; it was expected that they would all be promoted: in any event Holton would be.

[Pg 56]

‚ÄúNo, it doesn‚Äôt make a bit of difference.‚Äù

Robert Holton smiled. He had small white teeth and an agreeable smile which Kuppelton resented. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs good news for me. I haven‚Äôt been here very long you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, yes, I know,‚Äù and Kuppelton laughed loudly to show that he was friendly and that it made no difference to him who was promoted.

He glanced toward the windows. Mr Murphy caught his eye and motioned to him. Quickly Richard Kuppelton got to his feet and walked across the room to the railing. He was careful not to let the gate slam when he came into Mr Murphy‚Äôs presence.

‚ÄúYes, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúI just wanted to check with you on that aircraft stock report. I just wanted to make sure it was coming along well.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôve been working on it right along, Mr Murphy. They‚Äôll start typing it up tomorrow.‚Äù

Murphy compressed his lips and nodded slowly. ‚ÄúMr Golden was asking for it. I wanted to be sure, Dick.‚Äù

Kuppelton was suddenly glad that Mr Murphy had called him by his first name. He did this only when he was well pleased or when he wanted something.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs been quite a job getting those things together but I finally ... got them together.‚Äù

‚ÄúI know how it is. How‚Äôs your family these days?‚Äù

‚ÄúThey‚Äôre pretty well. My mother‚Äôs been better. Her legs don‚Äôt bother her so much now.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs good. Arthritis is pretty bad. I had a grandmother who had it once.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs pretty bad,‚Äù agreed Richard Kuppelton.

They both paused and wondered what to say next. Kuppelton[Pg 57] began to edge toward the gate. Murphy stood up. ‚ÄúLet me see that thing as soon as you get it done.‚Äù

‚ÄúI certainly will.‚Äù

Mr Murphy turned to Caroline who was typing at her desk. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going to be in conference for a while,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúTake care of the calls, will you?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, Mr Murphy.‚Äù

‚ÄúBig conference?‚Äù asked Kuppelton when Murphy had gone.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know,‚Äù said Caroline and she stopped typing. ‚ÄúThey were talking about it. Something to do with policy, I think.‚Äù

Caroline got up from her desk and stretched. She had nice slim legs, Kuppelton noticed. He wondered if his mother would like her. It was important to him to have his mother like his future wife—if he ever had one. She had been wonderful about the other girls he had liked but somehow they had never been quite what she thought his wife should be. He was her favorite son and he could not disappoint her, naturally.

‚ÄúI guess that leaves me out,‚Äù he said wearily, hoping she would give him some good news.

‚ÄúWell, I wouldn‚Äôt worry too much,‚Äù she said, a little coldly he thought, ‚Äúyou‚Äôve got a good job now.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, you‚Äôre right about that,‚Äù he said emphatically.

‚ÄúOh, I know I am. Bob‚Äôs the fair-haired boy these days,‚Äù she added.

‚ÄúI expect he is.‚Äù

Caroline walked to the window and looked down at the crowded street. ‚ÄúThere really are a lot of people in this town,‚Äù she said in a distant voice.

[Pg 58]

‚ÄúThere sure are.‚Äù

‚ÄúDo you ever wonder about all those people ... down there?‚Äù

This was the sort of talk that made Richard Kuppelton nervous. He hated it when people started asking him vague questions to which there were no sensible answers. ‚ÄúNo, I can‚Äôt say that I do.‚Äù

She turned around and looked at him then, looked at him rather sadly, he thought. ‚ÄúI‚Äôve got work to do,‚Äù was all she said.

‚ÄúSee you, Caroline.‚Äù

Robert Holton was leaning back in his chair.

‚ÄúPretty dull, isn‚Äôt it?‚Äù commented Dick.

‚ÄúThe army was a lot duller.‚Äù

‚ÄúI thought that was one thing that it wasn‚Äôt ... dull.‚Äù

Robert Holton chuckled. ‚ÄúThis is a lot better.‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you miss moving around?‚Äù

He paused before he replied and Kuppelton wondered what the truth really was; however, Robert Holton only said, ‚ÄúNo, no, I like staying in one place.‚Äù

Richard Kuppelton turned back to his books of figures. He wondered helplessly, as he wrote, how anyone could be as deceitful as Robert Holton. It was obvious to him that Holton would get the job he was to have gotten and he certainly could not get this job without being deceitful. Richard Kuppelton was worried about this. He was also worried because he found himself hating Robert Holton and his mother would never have approved of that.

[Pg 59]

Chapter Five

The ulcer was the most important thing.

After the ulcer his wife, and then his job, and finally his children. These were Mr Murphy‚Äôs interests. At the moment the ulcer was more important to him than all the others together.

Ever since Mr Murphy could remember, he had had pains in his stomach. Not really bad pains: just unpleasant sensations. In recent years this had gotten worse. A month before, a doctor examined him and said that he had an ulcer. The doctor was very serious and there was talk of further tests. Then Mr Murphy read a picture magazine article on cancer.

He did not suspect cancer: he knew. The doctor, although he had been rather grave, had said nothing about cancer, but Mr Murphy was confident he had it. He had tried to do everything right, to cure himself with bicarbonate of soda and other medicines but the pains not only didn‚Äôt go away but they got worse when he thought about them.

He pushed his fist into his stomach for a moment and felt the pain under his fingers. He cursed himself for having gone to the party the night before.

As he walked through his office he wished that he were[Pg 60] home in bed. It would have been harder, of course, to stay home, because his wife was not very good with an illness. She had a tendency to become hysterical if she had to do anything unusual. No, it was better to be here at the office. To be here even if he was dying. This last thought made him uncomfortable and he put it out of his mind.

He looked at his watch—eleven-fifteen. The meeting would begin soon. Mr Golden insisted that all meetings begin on time.

Mr Murphy left his office. As he walked through the rooms he was pleased to have everyone speak to him politely. He was a person of importance here and he had become this all by himself with no help from anyone; practically no help.

The executive offices were larger and better decorated than the other offices. There were several uniform rooms where the vice-presidents (they used to be partners but Mr Golden had changed that) sat at big desks and received clients and dictated letters and did other things. Then there was the anteroom. This was a small room with red leather couches, a receptionist, some modern lamps and two portraits on the walls. These paintings were of Mr Heywood and Mr Golden. Beyond the anteroom was the boardroom.

The receptionist smiled at Mr Murphy. He smiled back at her and sat down in one of the red leather couches. Two minor vice-presidents were also seated and waiting. They greeted him soberly.

‚ÄúNice morning,‚Äù said the younger of the vice-presidents; he had been a lieutenant commander in the navy.

‚ÄúCertainly is,‚Äù said Mr Murphy.

‚ÄúI understood we‚Äôre in for a cold winter,‚Äù commented the[Pg 61] older of the two vice-presidents; he had been a commander in the navy.

‚ÄúNothing like a real old-fashioned Christmas,‚Äù said Mr Murphy in a smooth low voice. He was conscious of a difference in their voices. His own voice sounded rough to him while their voices were always smooth and almost British. He had noticed these differences before but there was nothing much he could do about them. In the front office he always felt less important because of this difference, and because of this and other things, too, he was made to feel an outsider.

The vice-presidents then talked in their cultured near-British voices about a certain college football game. Mr Murphy lay back in his red couch and wondered if perhaps he should drink more milk. That was good for ulcers; but nothing was good for cancer. He shuddered.

A few more vice-presidents and section heads came into the anteroom. They talked and laughed together and Oliver L. Murphy talked and laughed with them.

There was a buzz and everyone stopped talking. The receptionist looked up from her desk. ‚ÄúThey‚Äôre ready,‚Äù she said.

The men walked into the boardroom of Heywood and Golden.

A long room, with indirect lighting, thick carpets, and a long table with armchairs around it: this was the boardroom. On the walls were charts of stocks and trends.

Mr Heywood was sitting at one end of the table and Mr Golden was sitting at the other end of the table. Murphy sat down on the left of Mr Heywood. This was his usual seat.

‚ÄúHello, Oliver,‚Äù said Mr Heywood cheerily.

[Pg 62]

‚ÄúHello, Mr Heywood.‚Äù Murphy was suddenly glad, glad that Mr Heywood had called him by his first name; he did this only when he was well-pleased, or wanted something.

Oliver L. Murphy leaned back in his leather armchair. Mr Heywood sat rather limply in his own chair at the head of the table. He waited for the others to be seated.

Lawrence Heywood was a gentleman. He had a large estate in Maryland and he collected prints; he had had three wives and a number of children and, generally, he had managed to do everything in a large but tasteful manner.

He was a tall man in his late forties. Completely bald, his neat round head shone pinkly under the indirect lights. His face was smooth and neat and looked as if he had never worried in his life. His voice was not near-British like his vice-presidents: it was British. He had gone to school in Massachusetts which explained a lot of it, thought Murphy.

Mr Heywood did everything properly. He had inherited a lot of money. It seemed as if every year a new relative would die and leave more money to him. His three wives had all been beautiful and that was another thing to be said for him—he knew how to choose women. Mr Murphy wondered what it would be like to marry a beautiful woman.

‚ÄúHow‚Äôs that new man in your office?‚Äù asked Mr Heywood suddenly.

‚ÄúYou mean Holton? He‚Äôs doing very well.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm glad to hear it. We have a mutual friend,‚Äù and Mr Heywood laughed gently at the thought.

‚ÄúIs that right? He‚Äôs got a good background, I guess,‚Äù said Murphy.

‚ÄúI expect so. I used to know his mother. She was a very[Pg 63] attractive woman twenty years ago. She married...‚Äù Mr Heywood decided not to reminisce in front of Murphy.

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs worked in my section, in the office, just fine.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs good. I don‚Äôt know him myself but I have some plans for him. We‚Äôre going to the same party tonight.‚Äù Mr Heywood laughed gently again. ‚ÄúPerhaps we‚Äôll get to know each other. It‚Äôs so hard ever getting to know employees in the office,‚Äù sighed Mr Heywood. ‚ÄúI rather wish there weren‚Äôt so many of them sometimes.‚Äù

‚ÄúI know just how it is.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe going to call this meeting together?‚Äù It was Mr Golden‚Äôs high voice from the other end of the table.

‚ÄúCertainly, Ben,‚Äù said Mr Heywood. ‚ÄúWe‚Äôll start right now.‚Äù He picked up a black ebony gavel and tapped lightly, apologetically with it. The men stopped talking. ‚ÄúNow, let‚Äôs see,‚Äù began Mr Heywood.

‚ÄúThe Steel account, that‚Äôs the big thing we‚Äôre going to talk about,‚Äù said Mr Golden.

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right.‚Äù Mr Heywood sounded bored. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right. Well, gentlemen, it seems that we have a problem.‚Äù

Mr Murphy relaxed in his chair. Mr Heywood‚Äôs voice, gentle and cultured, came to him soothingly. The Steel account was of no interest to Mr Murphy; in fact, these conferences were generally of no interest to him. He was just there to talk about Statistics.

He played with papers in front of him. The voice of Mr Heywood flowed about him. He was lost in a slow current of polite vowels. The pain in his stomach was, for the time, gone.

Mr Heywood spoke of the market, of stocks and shares, of the state of the Union. He spoke convincingly because his[Pg 64] manner was convincing and, also, because his ideas and facts had been given him by many clever men.

Mr Golden sat at his end of the table and listened. He sat there very straight, his little mouth set in a soft line of pseudo-firmness. His small hands drummed on the table and his eyes glanced about the room. His eyes were always in motion. The fear of a thousand years was in Mr Golden‚Äôs eyes.

From time to time he interrupted. Mr Heywood would pause and listen; then, when the other had finished, he would continue in his gentle voice to tell the others what clever men had told him about Steel, and the men, whose livings depended upon him, listened respectfully to their ideas.

Mr Murphy observed these things as he sat in his chair. He felt less important in these conferences but he did feel secure. Here in the boardroom he felt himself to be a part of something large and opulent—of American Business. This thought was comforting as well as sobering. There was no security in the world to equal that of belonging. It made no difference to what one belonged just as long as one was a part of something big and secure. And what, Oliver Murphy asked himself, could be bigger or more secure than Business? He saw these things clearly because he had a philosopher‚Äôs mind and the Celt‚Äôs ability to envisage life in a clear perspective. He could, he knew, see the trees as well as the forest. That was what made him different from the others. They felt, perhaps, that they belonged, but he knew.

Then the ulcer began to bother him.

He no longer was conscious of Mr Heywood‚Äôs voice. The only thing of importance now was the dull pain in his stomach.[Pg 65] He moved uneasily in his chair. He pushed a hand into his stomach. This helped a little. The pain shifted slightly. He followed it with his hand, his fingers pressing gently into the pain.

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll want complete figures on the rise and fall of Arizona Zinc during the past five years.‚Äù

This was said by Mr Heywood. It registered in Mr Murphy‚Äôs mind but he didn‚Äôt respond for a moment.

‚ÄúYou‚Äôll have those figures for us next meeting, won‚Äôt you?‚Äù Heywood asked, irritation in his voice.

‚ÄúCertainly, Mr Heywood,‚Äù said Murphy. He sat up straight and Mr Heywood nodded to him and then continued to talk.

Oliver Murphy listened carefully to everything said. He was beginning to sweat from the pain and the fear (more fear than pain, he told himself) but still he strained to hear every word and, slowly, as he listened, magic took place and the pain went away.

At last, when certain decisions had been made, Mr Heywood adjourned the meeting.

Murphy stood up. He felt better now. He wondered if perhaps he might not be mistaken about the cancer.

‚ÄúOh, Murphy.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, Mr Heywood?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat fellow in your office, that Holton, you think he‚Äôs quite efficient?‚Äù

‚ÄúI do.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wonder,‚Äù said Mr Heywood hesitantly, ‚ÄúI wonder how he might work out as one of our customers‚Äô men. Dealing with the public, all that sort of thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe‚Äôd probably do that very well.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou could afford to lose him?‚Äù

[Pg 66]

‚ÄúOh, yes, I think so.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wish,‚Äù said Mr Heywood petulantly, ‚Äúthat I knew him better. It‚Äôs terrible having so little contact with the office people.‚Äù

‚ÄúI could send him in to see you.‚Äù

‚ÄúGood Lord, no! I wouldn‚Äôt know what to say. I‚Äôll wait and see him tonight at Mrs Stevanson‚Äôs.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen do you think you‚Äôll change him over?‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, I don‚Äôt know. If I think he has the suitable, ah, temperament, we might change him this week.‚Äù

‚ÄúI know he‚Äôll be really tickled to hear this.‚Äù

‚ÄúI expect so.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow is Mrs Heywood?‚Äù asked Murphy politely.

‚ÄúShe‚Äôs fine, thank you,‚Äù said Mr Heywood blankly. Trouble, decided Murphy. The third Mrs Heywood seemed to be following the previous Mrs Heywoods.

‚ÄúWell...‚Äù said Murphy and he mumbled words to himself as he walked toward the door. Mr Heywood stared vacantly at him as he left.

Mr Murphy felt well when he was in motion. Walking with great dignity from office to office, conscious of the eyes of others upon him, was good for him. Aware of being a symbol of success he forgot his pains and some of his worries.

As he went into the Statistical office he could feel the atmosphere change. The clerks and typists became busy.

Mr Murphy went to his desk. ‚ÄúAny calls?‚Äù he asked.

Caroline shook her head. When she shook, her breasts quivered slightly. Mr Murphy noticed this and his stomach constricted with pain. Emotion was bad for him, according to the doctors. He looked away and tried to think of something else.

[Pg 67]

‚ÄúNo, there weren‚Äôt any calls. Some memorandums came in from the other sections but that was all.‚Äù

‚ÄúAny letters?‚Äù He thought of his family.

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù Caroline sounded surprised. ‚ÄúRight there on your desk. Right where I always put them.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, yes.‚Äù Mr Murphy sat down at his desk and looked at the pile of neat businesslike envelopes. He had no desire to open them.

Caroline typed rhythmically at her desk.

‚ÄúSay, Caroline....‚Äù

She stopped and looked at him.

‚ÄúTell Holton to step over here, will you?‚Äù

‚ÄúSure, Mr Murphy.‚Äù She got up and went through the gate and out into the office. He watched her legs as she walked determinedly to the other end of the room. He was almost pleased to feel the pain come flooding into his stomach. That would teach his stomach, he thought viciously.

The gate creaked and Robert Holton stood before him.

‚ÄúYou want to see me, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, yes, Holton. Sit down here. Over here on my left.‚Äù

Robert Holton sat down and looked expectant. Mr Murphy wondered for a moment why he had asked to see Holton. Then he remembered what Mr Heywood had said.

‚ÄúHow‚Äôs everything coming, Holton?‚Äù

‚ÄúJust fine, Mr Murphy.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, that‚Äôs good. Things have been going pretty well here. But I suppose you find things pretty dull after the army?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, no. I like this sort of work. I had enough moving around.‚Äù

‚ÄúI should think so. Well, that‚Äôs what most of us want, I[Pg 68] guess,‚Äù said Mr Murphy. ‚ÄúWe want to settle down. A lot of people say they don‚Äôt like routine but I think everybody does. It‚Äôs an important thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. I think it is.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere is,‚Äù said Mr Murphy, shutting his eyes for a moment to give the illusion of pondering, ‚Äúthere is security in working for a big house like Heywood and Golden.‚Äù He opened his eyes and looked directly at Holton. ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you feel that‚Äôs true?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, I hope so.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, it‚Äôs true.‚Äù Mr Murphy sighed and thought about going out to the country for a rest. A place that would have neither telephones nor mosquitoes. Most places had one or the other.

He looked at Robert Holton and wondered what he was thinking. He seemed a likeable young man. He was quiet and reserved and didn‚Äôt seem too aggressive. In fact that was probably a fault that Mr Murphy had not thought of. Holton was not a go-getter. He might lack initiative. That was why he was quiet and reserved. Or, as Mr Murphy finally thought, that might be a reason for his reserve.

‚ÄúTell me, Holton,‚Äù said Murphy, ‚Äúhave you had any ideas about, ah, your place here? I mean, what you would like to do. Naturally you wouldn‚Äôt be interested in staying here, in this department. With your education....‚Äù He permitted his voice to fade.

‚ÄúNo, I haven‚Äôt had any ideas; in fact, I haven‚Äôt thought too much about it. You see this is all pretty different from what it was like where I was in the army. I don‚Äôt suppose I‚Äôm quite used to the idea ... well, you know....‚Äù

[Pg 69]

‚ÄúI think I do. You would like to work in another department perhaps?‚Äù

Robert Holton looked at him. Mr Murphy could not tell what he was thinking for his face was relaxed and calm. ‚ÄúWell,‚Äù said Holton, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know. I don‚Äôt want to be out of my depth. ‚ÄúOf course, there‚Äôs a lot of work to knowing about stocks and bonds. You realize all the work that‚Äôs involved.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

‚ÄúPerhaps a place will be found for you in that department. It‚Äôs hard to say, though. With your, ah, background it shouldn‚Äôt be too hard. That is, if you have the stuff.‚Äù

‚ÄúI hope so.‚Äù

‚ÄúGood.‚Äù Mr Murphy watched Caroline typing. ‚ÄúI understand,‚Äù said Mr Murphy finally in a changed voice, ‚Äúthat you‚Äôre going out tonight.‚Äù

Robert Holton looked surprised. ‚ÄúWhat do you mean?‚Äù

‚ÄúMr Heywood said you and he were going to the same party.‚Äù

Holton smiled. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right, I‚Äôd forgotten. Mrs Stevanson‚Äôs giving a cocktail party. I guess that‚Äôs what he means.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt won‚Äôt hurt to be nice to him there,‚Äù said Mr Murphy with a laugh.

‚ÄúNo, I don‚Äôt suppose so.‚Äù

Mr Murphy looked at Holton and wondered what would become of him. If he had more initiative he might be a wealthy man because of his background (the important thing was background), but he would probably not go very[Pg 70] far. He might not even go as far as Mr Murphy had and Mr Murphy had been a success without background. Robert Holton didn‚Äôt look as though he cared to be a success.

‚ÄúWell, don‚Äôt let your night life interfere with business,‚Äù said Mr Murphy lightly.

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù said Holton rising, ‚ÄúI won‚Äôt.‚Äù

With a nod Mr Murphy dismissed him.

Mr Murphy watched Caroline absently as she typed. Her hair was rather long. It must be a nuisance to help her into a coat, he thought suddenly. That was something he hated to do. Whenever he helped a woman into a coat there was, first, a certain struggle to get her arms into the sleeves. Some women were better than others at this. And then, second, there was the problem of hair. If the woman had long hair it was inevitably caught inside the coat. This meant that her first motion was usually to free her hair and that involved a wild freeing and flinging of the hair which for anyone still posted behind her meant running a risk of becoming entangled. Mr Murphy wondered about these problems as he looked at Caroline‚Äôs long dark hair.

He had started to work on his letters (the ones in the business envelopes) when Richard Kuppelton appeared.

‚ÄúYes?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôve got the first part of that report here, the one on aircraft,‚Äù said Kuppelton.

‚ÄúYes?‚Äù Mr Murphy made himself sound cold and official.

‚ÄúWell, I wondered if you cared to look at them ... what I‚Äôve done so far, I mean.‚Äù

Mr Murphy looked at him for a moment without speaking. When Mr Murphy had first come to work for Heywood and Golden his then immediate boss had impressed him[Pg 71] greatly by just looking at him for several seconds at a time without speaking. Mr Murphy had adopted the mannerism and over the years had improved it until now he could be very frightening. He was that way now.

‚ÄúYou want me to do it for you?‚Äù he asked finally.

‚ÄúNo ... no, sir, I didn‚Äôt mean that. I just thought you would like to see what I got done.‚Äù Kuppelton was uncomfortable and Mr Murphy decided that he had done enough.

‚ÄúWhy, I‚Äôd be glad to look at it,‚Äù he said.

Kuppelton brightened. ‚ÄúThank you. I only wanted you to see the form I was using here. That was all. I‚Äôm making my conclusions in a slightly different way from usual and I thought....‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, I‚Äôll take a look at it.‚Äù

Kuppelton put a pile of papers down on Mr Murphy‚Äôs desk.

Mr Murphy nodded at him and Kuppelton left quickly. Mr Murphy felt much better after exercising his power. Poor Kuppelton was a good man in an office but he would never go very far because he didn‚Äôt have assurance. He would be promoted after the first of the year if Holton were moved out. That would make Kuppelton happy, which was a good thing. It wasn‚Äôt bad, thought Mr Murphy, to have contented people about you in a discontented world. He relaxed in his chair and then the pains started again.

This time the ache was about an inch below his belt and slightly toward the left (his appendix was on the right and, besides, his appendix was in good shape). The pain began to move toward the center. Quickly he pressed his fingers into the pain.

His heart beat rapidly and sweat formed on his face. If[Pg 72] the pain didn‚Äôt go away by the count of ten he would get up and take the special medicine his doctor had given him.

Frightened, Mr Murphy counted and the pain, not subject to this magic, did not go away.

[Pg 73]

Chapter Six

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs twelve o‚Äôclock,‚Äù Caroline said to Mr Murphy. ‚ÄúI think I‚Äôll go out to lunch, if that‚Äôs O.K.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, yes, Caroline.‚Äù

She thought he looked rather pale. She was about to ask him how he felt but she stopped herself, remembering how he disliked talking about his health. She had noticed that during the last year he had been taking a lot of medicine. Perhaps he was going to die. Caroline began to compose a little drama to herself. Mr Murphy had just collapsed across his desk and she had been the only one to keep a clear head....

‚ÄúYou coming, Caroline?‚Äù It was Robert Holton.

‚ÄúBe right there.‚Äù She arranged the papers on her desk, shut the drawers and joined Robert Holton outside the gate of the railing.

‚ÄúWhere‚Äôll we eat today?‚Äù asked Holton.

‚ÄúAt the restaurant, of course. Where did you think we would?‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, I don‚Äôt know.‚Äù He was smiling now and she wondered if he could have been trying to be funny; she could never be sure.

[Pg 74]

‚ÄúSometimes you don‚Äôt make sense,‚Äù said Caroline.

They were almost through the door when one of the secretaries called to Holton. ‚ÄúPhone, Bob.‚Äù

She waited for him at the door. He went over to his desk and answered the phone. He seemed excited, she noticed, and he talked very quickly. She wished she could hear what he was saying. Finally, he finished and joined her.

‚ÄúWho was that?‚Äù

‚ÄúAn old friend of mine.‚Äù

‚ÄúMan or woman?‚Äù

‚ÄúA guy I used to know. He just got in town. He comes from out West and I haven‚Äôt seen him for a couple of years.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou knew him in the army?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

They walked through the offices to the elevator and Holton pressed the button.

‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs he doing in town?‚Äù

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs just visiting. I‚Äôm going to see him this afternoon. He‚Äôs coming over here after lunch.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôll be nice. What does he look like?‚Äù She asked this gaily, hoping to have some effect on him. She didn‚Äôt, though.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know. He looks all right, I guess.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou certainly are good at description. Be sure to let me meet him.‚Äù

‚ÄúI will.‚Äù

The elevator stopped for them and they pushed into the lunch-going crowd. With a rush they descended to the street floor.

Outside the sun shone brightly above the street. The sky was a vivid blue and the air smelt clean in spite of the exhaust fumes and the people of the city. The day was warm.

[Pg 75]

They walked along the crowded street. Men of affairs with brief cases walked in and out of swinging glass doors. Younger men of affairs, wearing bowler hats and dark coats with darker velvet lapels, marched solemnly in the parade of business. The white-faced clerks squinted at the bright sun. Women secretaries walked together, admiring themselves in the windows. As they walked they talked to each other and to themselves.

‚ÄúWhat a nice day,‚Äù said Caroline, breathing deeply and coughing as the exhaust fumes tickled her throat.

‚ÄúMust be nice in the country,‚Äù commented Robert Holton.

‚ÄúNot you too?‚Äù Caroline laughed. ‚ÄúFirst Murphy and now you want to go out in the country.‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to go. I just said it must be pleasant there.‚Äù They crossed a street and he looked carefully to left and right and when they finally crossed the street the crowd had gone around them and the light was beginning to change again.

‚ÄúWhy do you take so long?‚Äù said Caroline disagreeably.

‚ÄúJust careful, that‚Äôs all.‚Äù

They walked in silence then. She was very conscious of his being beside her, of her arm being in his. This troubled Caroline, this awareness. She looked at Holton‚Äôs face as they walked down the crowded street. There was nothing in his face that she would like to have seen. This made her feel better because he was not the right person.

Over the high gray buildings was a narrow section of bright blue sky. It was almost too bright and contrasted strangely with the dingy buildings and the dark streets. Caroline watched the blue sky suspended upon the buildings. No clouds were in the sky but from time to time a bird would[Pg 76] circle in it. And, as she watched the sky, a large air liner, like a rigid bird, moved straightly eastward.

Caroline breathed deeply again, careful this time not to get the exhaust fumes too far down in her lungs. She coughed anyway.

Marjorie Ventusa looked through the plate-glass window at the street. She had been watching off and on for half an hour, waiting for Robert Holton to come.

Some days he would come in at twelve and other days at twelve-thirty, and then there had been certain days when he‚Äôd not come in at all and those were bad days for Marjorie Ventusa.

It was a few minutes after twelve when she saw him walking down the street, pushing through the crowd, a man different from all the others walking in the street. She frowned when she saw the pretty secretary with him. Marjorie hated this girl but she was helpless and could only hate all the others who seemed close to Robert Holton.

She pretended to be busy cleaning a table when they came in.

‚ÄúHello, Marjorie,‚Äù said Holton and he and Caroline came over to her table.

‚ÄúOh, hello, it‚Äôs you again.‚Äù She made herself sound matter-of-fact and bored, but her throat was suddenly full and she had to clear it before she could speak again. ‚ÄúWhat you going to eat today?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know,‚Äù said Holton and he and Caroline sat down at the table, across from each other. ‚ÄúWhat do you want, Caroline?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôd like to see a menu, I think,‚Äù said Caroline in a voice[Pg 77] that Marjorie Ventusa would like to have choked out of her.

‚ÄúHere,‚Äù said Marjorie and she handed them two white menus.

They studied the menus.

Many people were coming in and going out of the restaurant. All the tables were full now and there were people standing and waiting for tables. Some of her customers were beginning to look at her, waiting for her to take their order. She hoped Mrs Merrin would not notice how long she was taking with Robert Holton.

‚ÄúI think,‚Äù said Caroline, frowning a thin hair-wide frown, ‚ÄúI think I will have some tomato juice, and a lamb chop....‚Äù

‚ÄúNo more lamb chops,‚Äù said Marjorie, trying to keep the triumph from her voice.

The hair-wide frown became a scowl. ‚ÄúThen I‚Äôll have the veal.‚Äù

‚ÄúAny vegetables?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, the spinach.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou can have one other.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs all.‚Äù

And Marjorie thought, ‚Äúthe‚Äù spinach indeed. Why was it that when these people wanted to sound elegant they would talk about everything as ‚Äúthe‚Äù?

‚ÄúWhat do you want, Mr Holton?‚Äù She wished that she had the nerve to call him Bob, the right to call him that.

‚ÄúOh, I think I‚Äôll take the same.‚Äù

‚ÄúCoffee, tea, or milk?‚Äù She said the words as though they were one word.

They both asked for coffee and Marjorie went quickly out of the dining room and into the kitchen.

[Pg 78]

There was much more steam in the kitchen now than there had been at breakfast; as the day passed the kitchen got hotter, and steamier, and the cooks got more irritable and Mrs Merrin more nervous and Marjorie Ventusa would become tired and sad.

She called the new orders to the cook. Then she picked up two small glasses of tomato juice and put them on her tray. She fingered one of them a moment, thinking that soon he would be drinking from it. She enjoyed thinking of this, though it only made her desire stronger and her sadness greater.

She didn‚Äôt want to go back yet. She hoped Mrs Merrin would not come into the kitchen for a while.

But one of the swinging doors opened and Mrs Merrin walked into the kitchen. Quickly Marjorie picked up her tray and went back to the dining room.

Caroline and Robert Holton were talking seriously and Marjorie, because of the noise of voices in the dining room, couldn‚Äôt hear what they were saying.

They stopped talking as she came up to them.

‚ÄúHere you are,‚Äù said Marjorie Ventusa brightly, putting the glasses of tomato juice on the table.

Robert Holton smiled at her, showing his white even teeth.

‚ÄúHave you got a date for tonight?‚Äù asked Robert Holton.

‚ÄúYou know I always do.‚Äù

‚ÄúA sailor maybe?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm not saying.‚Äù

‚ÄúGet one who‚Äôll take you to Italy.‚Äù

This was cruel but Marjorie smiled and forgave him. She had not been joking when they spoke of Italy. She did not think it fair of him to say this in front of the pretty girl, but[Pg 79] Marjorie forgave him because he was young and because she felt about him in a certain way.

‚ÄúMaybe we‚Äôll go to Capri together,‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúIs it nice there?‚Äù

Richard Kuppelton looked at Ruth with disapproval. She was an aggressive woman and he was tired of aggressive women. His mother was that way. Caroline was more what he wanted. Caroline could act irritated with him and he would not mind. And she always smiled, even when she was angry; he could not feel that a woman who always smiled was aggressive. She had a mind of her own but then he could handle that. Eating veal, Richard Kuppelton felt he could handle anything.

Robert Holton finished eating. He sat back in his chair and yawned.

‚ÄúBored?‚Äù asked Caroline.

‚ÄúWell, I like that!‚Äù exclaimed Ruth. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôd think we weren‚Äôt good enough for him.‚Äù She said this in a way to let him know she was being humorous.

[Pg 82]

Richard Kuppelton was glad that Holton did not talk about the war. It made too great a difference between them and the women might have called attention to this difference.

He disliked Robert Holton because he was afraid of him. It was more than the threat to his job, much more than that. Caroline, whom Kuppelton wanted, seemed interested in him. He flattered himself that she was no more interested in Holton than she was in himself; still he was a threat.

Ruth was moving closer to Robert Holton now. Her thick curved lips, heavily painted a dark red, looked unpleasantly moist. Kuppelton had a desire to dry her mouth. Then Marjorie Ventusa did not like the dark-haired girl with the big nose who sat so close to him, but at least she was not a danger. She almost pitied this girl who had moved her chair so close to his that their legs were touching.

[Pg 84]

The other man was dull-looking and obviously interested in the girl with the blue eyes. Marjorie Ventusa wished him luck. Then, having thought these things about her customers, she walked over to their table.

‚ÄúReady for dessert?‚Äù asked Marjorie Ventusa cheerfully, trying not to look at Robert Holton.

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll have our check, please, Marjorie,‚Äù said Robert Holton and she liked the way he said her name.

‚ÄúCertainly.‚Äù She went to the cashier and had the four checks totalled. Then she came back.

‚ÄúBack to work,‚Äù said the blue-eyed girl with a sigh.

Chapter Seven

‚ÄúHere we are,‚Äù said Caroline.

He had dark eyes and bleached-looking hair which Caroline had always found attractive in men. His skin was rather pale for a Californian; all Californians had brown skin in her imagination. [Pg 89]

Trebling was surprised at the way Holton looked out of uniform.

‚ÄúSit down, Jim.‚Äù Holton pointed to a chair beside his desk. They both sat down. Trebling felt a little awkward. The office was too formal for him and he was not at ease.

[Pg 91]

As they talked, the words forming conventional patterns and hiding their real thoughts, Jim thought of the war.

‚ÄúYou remember the time we were in Florence?‚Äù

Holton said that he remembered it very well.

They spoke then of Florence and as they talked Jim Trebling began to remember many things.

The city had been liberated for several months. The war was almost over and Holton and Trebling were able to take a week‚Äôs leave: they went to Florence.

Parts of the city had been badly damaged. The old buildings on the Arno had been leveled in many places but the Ponte Vecchio was still there. These things had not been very important, however, because they had not gone to see antiques. They had gone to rest, to meet women, and to try to find enough liquor to get drunk on.

They stayed with a family outside of the town; they stayed in a place called Fiesole.

Trebling remembered the house clearly: long and rambling, dirty-white stucco with small iron balconies beneath the larger windows. A rock garden, dusty gray-green olive trees and an unearthly view of the valley in which was Florence.

The house belonged to a family named Bruno, friends of Robert Holton‚Äôs mother. They had invited the two of them to stay as long as they liked: in those days it was a good policy to have American soldiers in one‚Äôs home.

Robert Holton had liked a girl named Carla. Trebling had liked her too, but not as much as Holton did. He remembered one night when the three had sat on the terrace, watching the city.

[Pg 92]

It was summer and the night was warm and vibrant. The city lights glittered in the valley-cup; the lights were golden and flickering and the river shone darkly.

They sat on a stone ledge, their feet dangling above the rock garden. Carla was between them; her hair was dark and her face pale. They sat like this, watching the lights of the city and listening to the sound of insects whirring in the night.

And Jim had said, embarrassed by the long silence, ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs so peaceful here.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy should this change again?‚Äù asked Carla, looking at him, trying to tell his expression in the dark.

[Pg 95]

In the war he had been considered wild. He had spent most of the time laughing at things. He had been easily bored and now he was changed.

‚ÄúIt must be nice to be out,‚Äù Trebling repeated, not knowing what else to say.

And Robert Holton explained to him in detail why it was so nice to be free.

Paris had been the most interesting place of all. They had spent two days there. Trebling had been very conscientious and had insisted that they see palaces and landmarks and they had actually tried to see a few but then Holton decided that there was not enough time for that. They met two[Pg 96] girls. Trebling could not remember their names; he could remember nothing about them except that they were rather pretty and claimed to be sisters.

The girls had suggested they go on a picnic. Holton had liked this idea and he managed to get some food from the mess officer of a near-by company. They took bicycles and drove out of Paris. Hand in hand the two couples walked between the misty trees. There was no underbrush here and the trees came up out of the stony, grass-covered ground, free and straight.

The two girls understood what was expected of them. His most vivid memory was not of the one he had but of Holton‚Äôs: a stocky, pink-faced girl. [Pg 97]

‚ÄúSay, Bob, do you remember those two girls from Paris?‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen was that?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm afraid I‚Äôve forgotten.‚Äù That was that.
